			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Sysco" title="Sysco">Sysco</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sisqo" class="mw-redirect" title="Sisqo">Sisqo</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Cisco" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Cisco_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Cisco (disambiguation)">Cisco (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Cisco Systems, Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> technology <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(company)" title="Conglomerate (company)">conglomerate</a> headquartered in <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose, California</a>, in the center of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>.  Cisco develops, manufactures and sells <a href="/wiki/Networking_hardware" title="Networking hardware">networking hardware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_equipment" title="Telecommunications equipment">telecommunications equipment</a> and other <a href="/wiki/High-technology" class="mw-redirect" title="High-technology">high-technology</a> services and products.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Sep-2012-10-K_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Sep-2012-10-K-3">[3]</a></sup> Through its numerous acquired subsidiaries, such as <a href="/wiki/OpenDNS" title="OpenDNS">OpenDNS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Webex" class="mw-redirect" title="Webex">Webex</a>, Jabber and <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Jasper" title="Cisco Jasper">Jasper</a>, Cisco specializes in specific tech markets, such as the <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_Things" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet of Things">Internet of Things</a> (IoT), <a href="/wiki/Internet_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet domain">domain security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_management" title="Energy management">energy management</a>. Cisco is incorporated in California.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p><table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">Cisco Systems, Inc.</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="logo" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg" class="image"><img alt="Cisco logo blue 2016.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg/220px-Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="116" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg/330px-Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg/440px-Cisco_logo_blue_2016.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="216" data-file-height="114"></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="logo" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Cisco_Systems_Headquarters_(Building_10),_Cisco_San_Jose_Main_Campus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cisco Systems Headquarters (Building 10), Cisco San Jose Main Campus.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Cisco_Systems_Headquarters_%28Building_10%29%2C_Cisco_San_Jose_Main_Campus.jpg/270px-Cisco_Systems_Headquarters_%28Building_10%29%2C_Cisco_San_Jose_Main_Campus.jpg" decoding="async" width="270" height="137" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="814"></a><div>Building 10 of the Cisco San Jose Main Campus</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_legal_entity_types_by_country" title="List of legal entity types by country">Type</a></div></th><td class="category" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">Public</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Ticker_symbol" title="Ticker symbol">Traded as</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">

srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/17px-Decrease2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/22px-Decrease2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$97.79 billion</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">(2019)</span><sup id="cite_ref-10K_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Equity_(finance)" title="Equity (finance)">Total equity</a></span></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist nowrap"><ul><li><img alt="Decrease" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/11px-Decrease2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Decrease" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/17px-Decrease2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/22px-Decrease2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$33.57 billion</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">(2019)</span><sup id="cite_ref-10K_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">75,900 <span style="font-size:85%;">(2019)</span><sup id="cite_ref-10K_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_acquisitions_by_Cisco_Systems" title="List of acquisitions by Cisco Systems">List of acquisitions by Cisco Systems</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://cisco.com">cisco<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Cisco stock was added to the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> on June 8, 2009, and is also included in the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500" class="mw-redirect" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a> Index, the <a href="/wiki/Russell_1000_Index" title="Russell 1000 Index">Russell 1000 Index</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ-100" title="NASDAQ-100">NASDAQ-100</a> Index and the Russell 1000 Growth Stock Index.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p><p>Cisco Systems was founded in December 1984 by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bosack" title="Leonard Bosack">Leonard Bosack</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Lerner" title="Sandy Lerner">Sandy Lerner</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> computer scientists who had been instrumental in connecting computers at Stanford. They pioneered the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">local area network (LAN)</a> being used to connect geographically disparate computers over a multiprotocol <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">router system</a>. By the time the company went public in 1990, Cisco had a market capitalization of $224 million. By the end of the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a> in the year 2000, Cisco had a more than $500 billion market capitalization.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">[6]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1984.E2.80.931995:_Origins_and_initial_growth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1984–1995:_Origins_and_initial_growth">1984–1995: Origins and initial growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1984–1995: Origins and initial growth" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_(AGS)_router_(1986)_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_%28AGS%29_router_%281986%29_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg/220px-Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_%28AGS%29_router_%281986%29_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 147px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_%28AGS%29_router_%281986%29_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg/220px-Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_%28AGS%29_router_%281986%29_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="147" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cisco_Advanced_Gateway_Server_(AGS)_router_(1986)_-_Computer_History_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cisco's first router, the Advanced Gateway Server (AGS) router (1986)</div></div></div>
<p>Cisco Systems was founded in December 1984 by <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Lerner" title="Sandy Lerner">Sandy Lerner</a>, a director of computer facilities for the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Graduate_School_of_Business" title="Stanford Graduate School of Business">Stanford University Graduate School of Business</a>.  Lerner partnered with her husband, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bosack" title="Leonard Bosack">Leonard Bosack</a>, who was in charge of the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> computer science department's computers.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p><p>Cisco's initial product has roots in Stanford University's campus technology. In the early 1980s students and staff at Stanford; including Bosack, used technology on the campus to link all of the school's computer systems to talk to one another, creating a box that functioned as a multiprotocol router called the "Blue Box".<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> The Blue Box used software that was originally written at Stanford by research engineer <a href="/wiki/William_Yeager" title="William Yeager">William Yeager</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> Due to the underlying architecture, and its ability to scale well, Yeager's well-designed invention became a key to Cisco's early success.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p><p>In 1985, Bosack and Stanford employee Kirk Lougheed began a project to formally network Stanford's campus.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> They adapted Yeager's software into what became the foundation for <a href="/wiki/Cisco_IOS" title="Cisco IOS">Cisco IOS</a>, despite Yeager's claims that he had been denied permission to sell the Blue Box commercially. On July 11, 1986, Bosack and Lougheed were forced to resign from Stanford and the university contemplated filing criminal complaints against Cisco and its founders for the theft of its software, hardware designs, and other intellectual properties.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> In 1987, Stanford licensed the router software and two computer boards to Cisco.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> In addition to Bosack, Lerner, Lougheed, Greg Satz (a programmer), and Richard Troiano (who handled sales), completed the early Cisco team.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-8">[8]</a></sup> The company's first CEO was Bill Graves, who held the position from 1987 to 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-Acquisition_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acquisition-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1988, <a href="/wiki/John_Morgridge" title="John Morgridge">John Morgridge</a> was appointed CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p><p>The name "Cisco" was derived from the city name <i>San Francisco</i>, which is why the company's engineers insisted on using the lower case "cisco" in its early years. The logo is intended to depict the two towers of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gate_Bridge" title="Golden Gate Bridge">Golden Gate Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p><p>On February 16, 1990, Cisco Systems went public with a market capitalization of $224 million, and was listed on the <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a> stock exchange. On August 28, 1990, Lerner was fired. Upon hearing the news, her husband Bosack resigned in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>Although Cisco was not the first company to develop and sell dedicated network nodes,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> it was one of the first to sell commercially successful routers supporting multiple network protocols.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Classical, CPU-based architecture of early Cisco devices coupled with flexibility of operating system <a href="/wiki/Cisco_IOS" title="Cisco IOS">IOS</a> allowed for keeping up with evolving technology needs by means of frequent software upgrades. Some popular models of that time (such as <a href="/wiki/Cisco_2500" class="mw-redirect" title="Cisco 2500">Cisco 2500</a>) managed to stay in production for almost a decade virtually unchanged. The company was quick to capture the emerging service provider environment, entering the SP market with product lines such as Cisco 7000 and Cisco 8500.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>Between 1992 and 1994, Cisco acquired several companies in <a href="/wiki/Ethernet_switch" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethernet switch">Ethernet switching</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kalpana_(company)" title="Kalpana (company)">Kalpana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Grand Junction<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and most notably, <a href="/wiki/Mario_Mazzola" title="Mario Mazzola">Mario Mazzola</a>'s Crescendo Communications,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> which together formed the <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Catalyst" title="Cisco Catalyst">Catalyst</a> business unit. At the time, the company envisioned <a href="/wiki/Network_layer" title="Network layer">layer 3</a> routing and <a href="/wiki/Data_link_layer" title="Data link layer">layer 2</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ethernet" title="Ethernet">Ethernet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Token_Ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Token Ring">Token Ring</a>) switching as complementary functions of different intelligence and architecture—the former was slow and complex, the latter was fast but simple. This philosophy dominated the company's product lines throughout the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>In 1995, <a href="/wiki/John_Morgridge" title="John Morgridge">John Morgridge</a> was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/John_T._Chambers" title="John T. Chambers">John T. Chambers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Sep-1995-PRE-14A_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Sep-1995-PRE-14A-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1996.E2.80.932005:_Internet_and_silicon_intelligence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1996–2005:_Internet_and_silicon_intelligence">1996–2005: Internet and silicon intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1996–2005: Internet and silicon intelligence" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg/220px-John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4096" data-file-height="2810"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 151px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg/220px-John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="151" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_T._Chambers_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_T._Chambers" title="John T. Chambers">John T. Chambers</a> led Cisco as its CEO between 1995 and 2015. (Pictured at 2010 <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Davos" title="Davos">Davos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Internet_Protocol" title="Internet Protocol">Internet Protocol</a> (IP) became widely adopted in the mid-to-late 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-21">[21]</a></sup> Cisco introduced products ranging from modem access shelves (AS5200) to core <a href="/wiki/Cisco_12000" title="Cisco 12000">GSR</a> routers, making them a major player in the market.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-21">[21]</a></sup> In late March 2000, at the height of the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a>, Cisco became the most valuable company in the world, with a <a href="/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">market capitalization</a> of more than $500 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> As of July 2014, with a market cap of about US$129 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> it was still one of the most valuable companies.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p><p>The perceived complexity of programming routing functions in silicon led to the formation of several startups determined to find new ways to process IP and <a href="/wiki/MPLS" class="mw-redirect" title="MPLS">MPLS</a> packets entirely in hardware and blur boundaries between routing and switching. One of them, <a href="/wiki/Juniper_Networks" title="Juniper Networks">Juniper Networks</a>, shipped their first product in 1999 and by 2000 chipped away about 30% from Cisco SP Market share. In response, Cisco later developed homegrown <a href="/wiki/ASIC" class="mw-redirect" title="ASIC">ASICs</a> and fast processing cards for GSR routers and <a href="/wiki/Catalyst_6500" title="Catalyst 6500">Catalyst 6500</a> switches. In 2004, Cisco also started the migration to new high-end hardware <a href="/wiki/Cisco_CRS-1" class="mw-redirect" title="Cisco CRS-1">CRS-1</a> and software architecture <a href="/wiki/IOS-XR" class="mw-redirect" title="IOS-XR">IOS-XR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2006.E2.80.932012:_The_Human_Network"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2006–2012:_The_Human_Network">2006–2012: The Human Network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2006–2012: The Human Network" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg/220px-Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="434"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 147px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg/220px-Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="147" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dmitry_Medvedev_in_the_United_States_23_June_2010-2.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian President <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev" title="Dmitry Medvedev">Dmitry Medvedev</a> and California Gov. <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a> at Cisco, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As part of a rebranding campaign in 2006, Cisco Systems adopted the shortened name "Cisco" and created "The Human Network" advertising campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-human-network_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-human-network-27">[27]</a></sup> These efforts were meant to make Cisco a "household" brand—a strategy designed to support the low-end Linksys products and future consumer products.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>On the more traditional business side, Cisco continued to develop its routing, switching and security portfolio. The quickly growing importance of <a href="/wiki/Ethernet" title="Ethernet">Ethernet</a> also influenced the company's product lines. Limits of <a href="/wiki/Cisco_IOS" title="Cisco IOS">IOS</a> and aging Crescendo architecture also forced Cisco to look at merchant silicon in the carrier Ethernet segment. This resulted in a new <a href="/wiki/ASR9000" title="ASR9000">ASR9000</a> product family intended to consolidate the company's carrier ethernet and subscriber management business around <a href="/wiki/EZchip_Semiconductor" title="EZchip Semiconductor">EZChip</a>-based hardware and <a href="/wiki/IOS-XR" class="mw-redirect" title="IOS-XR">IOS-XR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>Throughout the mid-2000s, Cisco also built a significant presence in India, establishing its Globalization Centre East in <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a> for $1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Segal2011_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segal2011-28">[28]</a></sup> Cisco also expanded into new markets by acquisition—one example being a 2009 purchase of mobile specialist <a href="/wiki/Starent_Networks" title="Starent Networks">Starent Networks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p><p>Cisco continued to be challenged by both domestic competitors <a href="/wiki/Alcatel-Lucent" title="Alcatel-Lucent">Alcatel-Lucent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juniper_Networks" title="Juniper Networks">Juniper Networks</a> and an overseas competitor <a href="/wiki/Huawei" title="Huawei">Huawei</a>. Due to lower-than-expected profit in 2011, Cisco reduced annual expenses by $1 billion. The company cut around 3,000 employees with an early-retirement program who accepted a buyout and planned to eliminate as many as 10,000 jobs (around 14 percent of the 73,400 total employees before curtailment).<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> During the 2011 analyst call, Cisco's CEO John Chambers called out several competitors by name,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> including Juniper and HP.
</p><p>On July 24, 2012, Cisco received approval from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> to acquire NDS (a TV software developer) for US$5 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In 2013, Cisco sold its Linksys home-router unit to Belkin International Inc., signaling a shift to sales to businesses rather than consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_day">Present day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Present day" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cavaco_na_California.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Cavaco_na_California.jpg/200px-Cavaco_na_California.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="261" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="446"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 261px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Cavaco_na_California.jpg/200px-Cavaco_na_California.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="261" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cavaco_na_California.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Portuguese President <a href="/wiki/An%C3%ADbal_Cavaco_Silva" title="Aníbal Cavaco Silva">Cavaco Silva</a>, former Cisco CEO <a href="/wiki/John_T._Chambers" title="John T. Chambers">John Chambers</a> and Cisco Senior Director of Innovation <a href="/wiki/Helder_Antunes" title="Helder Antunes">Helder Antunes</a>, during the 2011 presidential visit to the US.</div></div></div>
<p>On July 23, 2013, Cisco Systems announced a definitive agreement to acquire <a href="/wiki/Sourcefire" title="Sourcefire">Sourcefire</a> for $2.7 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> On August 14, 2013, Cisco Systems announced it would cut 4,000 jobs from its workforce, which was roughly 6%, starting in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> At the end of 2013, Cisco announced poor revenue due to depressed sales in emerging markets, caused by economic uncertainty and by fears of the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> planting backdoors in its products.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2014, Cisco announced funding for early-stage firms to focus on the Internet of Things. The investment fund was allocated to investments in IoT accelerators and startups such as <a href="/wiki/The_Alchemist_Accelerator" title="The Alchemist Accelerator">The Alchemist Accelerator</a>, Ayla Networks and <a href="/wiki/EVRYTHNG" title="EVRYTHNG">EVRYTHNG</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>  Later that year, the company announced it was laying off another 6,000 workers or 8% of its global workforce, as part of a second restructuring.<sup id="cite_ref-CiscoRestructure_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CiscoRestructure-39">[39]</a></sup> On November 4, 2014, Cisco announced an investment in <a href="/wiki/Stratoscale" title="Stratoscale">Stratoscale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p><p>On May 4, 2015, Cisco announced <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">CEO</a> and Chairman <a href="/wiki/John_Chambers_(CEO)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Chambers (CEO)">John Chambers</a> would step down as CEO on July 26, 2015, but remain chairman. <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Robbins" title="Chuck Robbins">Chuck Robbins</a>, senior vice president of worldwide sales &amp; operations and 17-year Cisco veteran, was announced as the next CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-newCEO_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newCEO-41">[41]</a></sup> On July 23, 2015, Cisco announced the divesture of its television set-top-box and <a href="/wiki/Cable_modem" title="Cable modem">cable modem</a> business to <a href="/wiki/Technicolor_SA" title="Technicolor SA">Technicolor SA</a> for $600 million, a division originally formed by Cisco's $6.9 billion purchase of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Atlanta" title="Scientific Atlanta">Scientific Atlanta</a>. The deal came as part of Cisco's gradual exit from the consumer market, and as part of an effort by Cisco's new leadership to focus on cloud-based products in enterprise segments. Cisco indicated that it would still collaborate with Technicolor on video products.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj-stbsale_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-stbsale-42">[42]</a></sup> On November 19, 2015, Cisco, alongside <a href="/wiki/ARM_Holdings" class="mw-redirect" title="ARM Holdings">ARM Holdings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/OpenFog_Consortium" title="OpenFog Consortium">OpenFog Consortium</a>, to promote interests and development in <a href="/wiki/Fog_computing" title="Fog computing">fog computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2016, Cisco invested in VeloCloud, a software-defined WAN (SD-WAN) start-up with a cloud offering for configuring and optimizing branch office networks. Cisco contributed to VeloCloud's $27 million Series C round, led by March Capital Partners.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2017, Cisco launched a cloud-based secure internet gateway, called Cisco Umbrella, to provide safe internet access to users who do not use their corporate networks or VPNs to connect to remote data centers.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Immediately after reporting their fourth-quarter earnings for 2017, Cisco's price-per-share value jumped by over 7%, while its <a href="/wiki/Earnings_per_share" title="Earnings per share">Earnings per share</a> Ratio increased from 60 to 61 cents per share, due in part to Cisco's outperformance of analyst expectations.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In September 2017, Chambers announced that he would step down from the executive chairman role at the end of his term on the board in December 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> On December 11, 2017, Robbins was elected to succeed Chambers as executive chairman while retaining his role as CEO, and Chambers was given the title of "Chairman Emeritus".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p><p>Reuters reported that "Cisco Systems Inc’s (CSCO.O) product revenue in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> grew 20 percent in 2017, ahead of Cisco’s technology product revenue growth in the other so-called BRIC countries of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p><p>On May 1, 2018, Cisco Systems agreed to buy AI-driven business intelligence startup Accompany for $270 million.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> As of June 2018, Cisco Systems ranked 444th on Forbes Global 2000 list, with $221.3 billion market cap.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p><p>In 2019, Cisco acquired CloudCherry, a customer experience management company, and Voicea, an artificial intelligence company.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2020, CEO David Goeckeler left to become CEO fo <a href="/wiki/Western_Digital" title="Western Digital">Western Digital Corp</a>. and was replaced by Todd Nightingale, head of Cisco Meraki.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance">Finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Finance" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<p>For the fiscal year 2018, Cisco reported earnings of US$0.1 billion, with an annual revenue of US$49.3 billion, an increase of 2.8% over the previous fiscal cycle. Cisco's shares traded at over $43 per share, and its market capitalization was valued at US$213.2 billion in September 2018. Low Net Income for fiscal year 2018 was attributed to a one time tax charge, that allowed Cisco to bring back capital from overseas. Cisco used this money it was able to bring back at a lower tax rate to fund share buybacks and acquisitions. 
</p>

<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_and_subsidiaries">Acquisitions and subsidiaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cisco_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Acquisitions and subsidiaries" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_acquisitions_by_Cisco_Systems" title="List of acquisitions by Cisco Systems">List of acquisitions by Cisco Systems</a></div>
<p>Cisco acquired a variety of companies to spin products and talent into the company. In 1995–1996 the company completed 11 acquisitions.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Several acquisitions, such as <a href="/wiki/Stratacom" class="mw-redirect" title="Stratacom">Stratacom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-fisher_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fisher-76">[76]</a></sup> were the biggest deals in the industry when they occurred.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the Internet boom in 1999, the company acquired <a href="/wiki/Cerent_Corporation" title="Cerent Corporation">Cerent Corporation</a>, a start-up company located in <a href="/wiki/Petaluma,_California" title="Petaluma, California">Petaluma, California</a>, for about US$7 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Aug-1999-8-K_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Aug-1999-8-K-77">[77]</a></sup> It was the most expensive acquisition made by Cisco to that date, and only the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Atlanta" title="Scientific Atlanta">Scientific Atlanta</a> has been larger.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Nov-2005-10-Q_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Nov-2005-10-Q-78">[78]</a></sup> In 1999 Cisco also acquired stake for $1 Billion in KPMG Consulting to enable establishing Internet firm Metrius founded by Keyur Patel of Fuse.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Several acquired companies have grown into $1Bn+ business units for Cisco, including LAN switching, Enterprise <a href="/wiki/Voice_over_Internet_Protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Voice over Internet Protocol">Voice over Internet Protocol</a> (VOIP) platform <a href="/wiki/Webex" class="mw-redirect" title="Webex">Webex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Home_networking" class="mw-redirect" title="Home networking">home networking</a>. The latter came as result of Cisco acquiring <a href="/wiki/Linksys" title="Linksys">Linksys</a> in 2003 and in 2010 was supplemented with new product line dubbed <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Valet" class="mw-redirect" title="Cisco Valet">Cisco Valet</a>.
</p><p>Cisco announced on January 12, 2005, that it would acquire <a href="/wiki/Airespace" title="Airespace">Airespace</a> for US$450 million to reinforce the wireless controller product lines.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p><p>Cisco announced on January 4, 2007, that it would buy <a href="/wiki/IronPort" title="IronPort">IronPort</a> in a deal valued at US$830 million<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and completed the acquisition on June 25, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IronPort" title="IronPort">IronPort</a> was best known for its IronPort AntiSpam, its SenderBase email reputation service and its email security appliances. Accordingly, <a href="/wiki/IronPort" title="IronPort">IronPort</a> was integrated into the Cisco Security business unit.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Ironport's Senderbase was renamed as Sensorbase to take account of the input into this database that other Cisco devices provide. SensorBase allows these devices to build a risk profile on IP addresses, therefore allowing risk profiles to be dynamically created on http sites and SMTP email sources.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p><p>Cisco announced on March 15, 2012, that it would acquire <a href="/wiki/NDS_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="NDS Group">NDS Group</a> for $5bn.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Mar-2012-8-K_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Mar-2012-8-K-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The transaction was completed on July 30, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-2013_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-2013-88">[88]</a></sup></p><p>In more recent merger deals, Cisco bought Starent Networks (a mobile packet core company) and Moto Development Group, a product design consulting firm that helped develop Cisco's Flip video camera.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Oct-2009-8-K_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Oct-2009-8-K-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-moto_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moto-90">[90]</a></sup> Also in 2010, Cisco became a key stakeholder in <i>e-Skills Week</i>. In March 2011, Cisco completed the acquisition of privately held network configuration and change management software company Pari Networks.<sup id="cite_ref-post_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-post-91">[91]</a></sup></p><p>Although many buy-ins (such as Crescendo Networks in 1993, <a href="/wiki/Tandberg" title="Tandberg">Tandberg</a> in 2010) resulted in acquisition of flagship technology to Cisco, many others have failed—partially or completely.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-92">[92]</a></sup> For instance, in 2010 Cisco occupied a meaningful share of the packet-optical market,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> revenues were still not on par with US$7 billion price tag paid in 1999 for <a href="/wiki/Cerent_Corporation" title="Cerent Corporation">Cerent</a>. Some of acquired technologies (such as Flip from Pure Digital) saw their product lines terminated.<sup id="cite_ref-Cisco-Nov-2009-10-Q_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cisco-Nov-2009-10-Q-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner"
